						<!-- firstHeading -->
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">Artificial intelligence</h1>
			<!-- /firstHeading -->
			<!-- bodyContent -->
			<div id="bodyContent">
								<!-- tagline -->
				<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
				<!-- /tagline -->
								<!-- subtitle -->
				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<!-- /subtitle -->
																<!-- jumpto -->
				<div id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to: <a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>,
					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<!-- /jumpto -->
								<!-- bodycontent -->
				<div lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="dablink">"AI" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Ai_(disambiguation)" title="Ai (disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect">Ai</a>.</div>
<div class="dablink">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_(disambiguation)" title="Artificial intelligence (disambiguation)">Artificial intelligence (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p><b>Artificial intelligence</b> (<b>AI</b>) is the <a href="/wiki/Intelligence" title="Intelligence">intelligence</a> of machines and the branch of <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> that aims to create it. AI textbooks define the field as "the study and design of intelligent agents"<sup id="cite_ref-Definition_of_AI_0-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Definition_of_AI-0"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> where an <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_agent" title="Intelligent agent">intelligent agent</a> is a system that perceives its environment and takes actions that maximize its chances of success.<sup id="cite_ref-Intelligent_agents_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intelligent_agents-1"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)">John McCarthy</a>, who coined the term in 1956,<sup id="cite_ref-Coining_of_the_term_AI_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coining_of_the_term_AI-2"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> defines it as "the science and engineering of making intelligent machines."<sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy.27s_definition_of_AI_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy.27s_definition_of_AI-3"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The field was founded on the claim that a central property of humans, intelligence—the <a href="/wiki/Sapience" title="Sapience" class="mw-redirect">sapience</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens" class="mw-redirect">Homo sapiens</a></i>—can be so precisely described that it can be simulated by a machine.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> This raises philosophical issues about the nature of the <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> and the ethics of creating artificial beings, issues which have been addressed by <a href="/wiki/History_of_AI#AI_in_myth.2C_fiction_and_speculation" title="History of AI" class="mw-redirect">myth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_in_fiction" title="Artificial intelligence in fiction">fiction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_AI" title="Philosophy of AI" class="mw-redirect">philosophy</a> since antiquity.<sup id="cite_ref-McCorduck.27s_thesis_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCorduck.27s_thesis-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Artificial intelligence has been the subject of optimism,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> but has also suffered <a href="/wiki/AI_winter" title="AI winter">setbacks</a><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> and, today, has become an essential part of the technology industry, providing the heavy lifting for many of the <a href="/wiki/Unsolved_problems_in_computer_science" title="Unsolved problems in computer science" class="mw-redirect">most difficult problems in computer science</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_widely_used_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_widely_used-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>AI research is highly technical and specialized, <i>deeply</i> divided into subfields that often fail in the task of communicating with each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Fragmentation_of_AI_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fragmentation_of_AI-9"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Subfields have grown up around particular institutions, the work of individual researchers, and the solution of specific problems, resulting in longstanding differences of opinion about how AI should be done and the application of widely differing tools. The central problems of AI include such traits as reasoning, knowledge, planning, learning, communication, perception and the ability to move and manipulate objects.<sup id="cite_ref-Problems_of_AI_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Problems_of_AI-10"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> General intelligence (or "<a href="/wiki/Strong_AI" title="Strong AI">strong AI</a>") is still among the field's long term goals.<sup id="cite_ref-General_intelligence_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-General_intelligence-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence" title="History of artificial intelligence">History of artificial intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Timeline of artificial intelligence">Timeline of artificial intelligence</a></div>
<p>Thinking machines and artificial beings appear in <a href="/wiki/Greek_myth" title="Greek myth" class="mw-redirect">Greek myths</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Talos" title="Talos">Talos</a> of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, the bronze robot of <a href="/wiki/Hephaestus" title="Hephaestus">Hephaestus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pygmalion_(mythology)" title="Pygmalion (mythology)">Pygmalion's</a> <a href="/wiki/Galatea_(mythology)" title="Galatea (mythology)">Galatea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_in_myth_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_in_myth-12"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Human likenesses believed to have intelligence were built in every major civilization: animated <a href="/wiki/Cult_image" title="Cult image">cult images</a> were worshipped in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a><sup id="cite_ref-Cult_images_as_artificial_intelligence_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cult_images_as_artificial_intelligence-13"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> and humanoid <a href="/wiki/Automaton" title="Automaton">automatons</a> were built by <a href="/wiki/King_Mu_of_Zhou#Automaton" title="King Mu of Zhou">Yan Shi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hero_of_Alexandria" title="Hero of Alexandria">Hero of Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Jazari" title="Al-Jazari">Al-Jazari</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Humanoid_automata_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Humanoid_automata-14"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> It was also widely believed that artificial beings had been created by <a href="/wiki/J%C4%81bir_ibn_Hayy%C4%81n" title="Jābir ibn Hayyān">Jābir ibn Hayyān</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judah_Loew" title="Judah Loew" class="mw-redirect">Judah Loew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paracelsus" title="Paracelsus">Paracelsus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Artificial_beings_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Artificial_beings-15"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> By the 19th and 20th centuries, artificial beings had become a common feature in fiction, as in <a href="/wiki/Mary_Shelley" title="Mary Shelley">Mary Shelley</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein">Frankenstein</a></i> or <a href="/wiki/Karel_%C4%8Capek" title="Karel Čapek">Karel Čapek</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/R.U.R._(Rossum%27s_Universal_Robots)" title="R.U.R. (Rossum's Universal Robots)" class="mw-redirect">R.U.R. (Rossum's Universal Robots)</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_in_early_science_fiction_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_in_early_science_fiction-16"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pamela_McCorduck" title="Pamela McCorduck">Pamela McCorduck</a> argues that all of these are examples of an ancient urge, as she describes it, "to forge the gods".<sup id="cite_ref-McCorduck.27s_thesis_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCorduck.27s_thesis-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Stories of these creatures and their fates discuss many of the same hopes, fears and <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Ethics of artificial intelligence">ethical concerns</a> that are presented by artificial intelligence.</p>
<p>Mechanical or <a href="/wiki/Formal_reasoning" title="Formal reasoning" class="mw-redirect">"formal" reasoning</a> has been developed by philosophers and mathematicians since antiquity. The study of logic led directly to the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">programmable digital electronic computer</a>, based on the work of mathematician <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a> and others. Turing's <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_computation" title="Theory of computation">theory of computation</a> suggested that a machine, by shuffling symbols as simple as "0" and "1", could simulate any conceivable act of mathematical deduction.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Formal_reasoning_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Formal_reasoning-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> This, along with concurrent discoveries in <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cybernetic" title="Cybernetic" class="mw-redirect">cybernetics</a>, inspired a small group of researchers to begin to seriously consider the possibility of building an electronic brain.<sup id="cite_ref-AI.27s_immediate_precursors_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI.27s_immediate_precursors-19"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The field of AI research was founded at <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Conferences" title="Dartmouth Conferences">a conference</a> on the campus of <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth College</a> in the summer of 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_conference_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_conference-20"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> The attendees, including <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)">John McCarthy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Minsky" title="Marvin Minsky">Marvin Minsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allen_Newell" title="Allen Newell">Allen Newell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Simon" title="Herbert Simon">Herbert Simon</a>, became the leaders of AI research for many decades.<sup id="cite_ref-Hegemony_of_the_Dartmouth_conference_attendees_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hegemony_of_the_Dartmouth_conference_attendees-21"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> They and their students wrote programs that were, to most people, simply astonishing:<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Computers were solving word problems in algebra, proving logical theorems and speaking English.<sup id="cite_ref-Golden_years_of_AI_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Golden_years_of_AI-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> By the middle of the 1960s, research in the U.S. was heavily funded by the <a href="/wiki/DARPA" title="DARPA">Department of Defense</a><sup id="cite_ref-AI_funding_in_the_60s_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_funding_in_the_60s-24"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> and laboratories had been established around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_in_England_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_in_England-25"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> AI's founders were profoundly optimistic about the future of the new field: <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Simon" title="Herbert Simon">Herbert Simon</a> predicted that "machines will be capable, within twenty years, of doing any work a man can do" and <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Minsky" title="Marvin Minsky">Marvin Minsky</a> agreed, writing that "within a generation&#160;... the problem of creating 'artificial intelligence' will substantially be solved".<sup id="cite_ref-Optimism_of_early_AI_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Optimism_of_early_AI-26"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>They had failed to recognize the difficulty of some of the problems they faced.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1974, in response to the criticism of <a href="/wiki/Sir_James_Lighthill" title="Sir James Lighthill" class="mw-redirect">Sir James Lighthill</a> and ongoing pressure from the US Congress to fund more productive projects, both the U.S. and British governments cut off all undirected exploratory research in AI. The next few years, when funding for projects was hard to find, would later be called the "<a href="/wiki/AI_winter" title="AI winter">AI winter</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-First_AI_winter_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-First_AI_winter-28"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 1980s, AI research was revived by the commercial success of <a href="/wiki/Expert_systems" title="Expert systems" class="mw-redirect">expert systems</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Expert_systems_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Expert_systems-29"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> a form of AI program that simulated the knowledge and analytical skills of one or more human experts. By 1985 the market for AI had reached over a billion dollars. At the same time, Japan's <a href="/wiki/Fifth_generation_computer" title="Fifth generation computer">fifth generation computer</a> project inspired the U.S and British governments to restore funding for academic research in the field.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_in_the_80s_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_in_the_80s-30"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> However, beginning with the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Lisp_Machine" title="Lisp Machine" class="mw-redirect">Lisp Machine</a> market in 1987, AI once again fell into disrepute, and a second, longer lasting <a href="/wiki/AI_winter" title="AI winter">AI winter</a> began.<sup id="cite_ref-Second_AI_winter_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Second_AI_winter-31"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1990s and early 21st century, AI achieved its greatest successes, albeit somewhat behind the scenes. Artificial intelligence is used for <a href="/wiki/Logistics" title="Logistics">logistics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Data_mining" title="Data mining">data mining</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis" title="Medical diagnosis">medical diagnosis</a> and many other areas throughout the technology industry.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_widely_used_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_widely_used-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> The success was due to several factors: the increasing computational power of computers (see <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore's law">Moore's law</a>), a greater emphasis on solving specific subproblems, the creation of new ties between AI and other fields working on similar problems, and a new commitment by researchers to solid mathematical methods and rigorous scientific standards.<sup id="cite_ref-Formal_methods_in_AI_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Formal_methods_in_AI-32"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 11 May 1997, <a href="/wiki/IBM_Deep_Blue" title="IBM Deep Blue" class="mw-redirect">Deep Blue</a> became the first computer chess-playing system to beat a reigning world chess champion, <a href="/wiki/Garry_Kasparov" title="Garry Kasparov">Garry Kasparov</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2005, a Stanford robot won the <a href="/wiki/DARPA_Grand_Challenge" title="DARPA Grand Challenge">DARPA Grand Challenge</a> by driving autonomously for 131 miles along an unrehearsed desert trail.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Two years later, a team from CMU won the <a href="/wiki/DARPA_Urban_Challenge" title="DARPA Urban Challenge" class="mw-redirect">DARPA Urban Challenge</a> by autonomously navigating 55 miles in an Urban environment while adhering to traffic hazards and all traffic laws.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> In February 2011, in a <a href="/wiki/Jeopardy!" title="Jeopardy!">Jeopardy!</a> <a href="/wiki/Quiz_show" title="Quiz show" class="mw-redirect">quiz show</a> exhibition match, <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Question_answering_system" title="Question answering system" class="mw-redirect">question answering system</a>, <a href="/wiki/Watson_(artificial_intelligence_software)" title="Watson (artificial intelligence software)" class="mw-redirect">Watson</a>, defeated the two greatest Jeopardy! champions, <a href="/wiki/Brad_Rutter" title="Brad Rutter">Brad Rutter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ken_Jennings" title="Ken Jennings">Ken Jennings</a>, by a significant margin.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The leading-edge definition of artificial intelligence <a href="/wiki/Research" title="Research">research</a> is changing over time. One pragmatic definition is: "AI research is that which computing scientists do not know how to do cost-effectively today." For example, in 1956 <a href="/wiki/Optical_character_recognition" title="Optical character recognition">optical character recognition</a> (OCR) was considered AI, but today, sophisticated OCR software with a context-sensitive <a href="/wiki/Spell_checker" title="Spell checker">spell checker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammar_checker" title="Grammar checker">grammar checker</a> software comes for free with most <a href="/wiki/Image_scanner" title="Image scanner">image scanners</a>. No one would any longer consider already-solved computing science problems like OCR "artificial intelligence" today.</p>
<p>Low-cost entertaining chess-playing software is commonly available for tablet computers. <a href="/wiki/DARPA" title="DARPA">DARPA</a> no longer provides significant funding for chess-playing computing system development. The <a href="/wiki/Kinect" title="Kinect">Kinect</a> which provides a 3D body–motion interface for the <a href="/wiki/Xbox_360" title="Xbox 360">Xbox 360</a> uses algorithms that emerged from lengthy AI research,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> but few consumers realize the technology source.</p>
<p>AI applications are no longer the exclusive domain of <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Defense" title="U.S. Department of Defense" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Department of Defense</a> R&amp;D, but are now common place consumer items and inexpensive intelligent toys.</p>
<p>In common usage, the term "AI" no longer seems to apply to off-the-shelf solved computing-science problems, which may have originally emerged out of years of AI research.<br /></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Problems">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Problems">Problems</span></h2>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Philosophy of artificial intelligence">Can a machine act intelligently?</a>" is still an <a href="/wiki/Open_problem" title="Open problem">open problem</a>. Taking "A machine can act intelligently" as a <a href="/wiki/Working_hypothesis" title="Working hypothesis">working hypothesis</a>, many researchers have attempted to build such a machine.</p>
<p>The general problem of simulating (or creating) intelligence has been broken down into a number of specific <a href="/wiki/History_of_AI#The_problems" title="History of AI" class="mw-redirect">sub-problems</a>. These consist of particular traits or capabilities that researchers would like an intelligent system to display. The traits described below have received the most attention.<sup id="cite_ref-Problems_of_AI_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Problems_of_AI-10"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Deduction, reasoning, problem solving">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Deduction.2C_reasoning.2C_problem_solving">Deduction, reasoning, problem solving</span></h3>
<p>Early AI researchers developed algorithms that imitated the step-by-step reasoning that humans use when they solve puzzles or make logical deductions.<sup id="cite_ref-Reasoning_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reasoning-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> By the late 1980s and '90s, AI research had also developed highly successful methods for dealing with <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty" title="Uncertainty">uncertain</a> or incomplete information, employing concepts from <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probability</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Uncertain_reasoning_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uncertain_reasoning-39"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>For difficult problems, most of these algorithms can require enormous computational resources — most experience a "<a href="/wiki/Combinatorial_explosion" title="Combinatorial explosion">combinatorial explosion</a>": the amount of memory or computer time required becomes astronomical when the problem goes beyond a certain size. The search for more efficient problem-solving algorithms is a high priority for AI research.<sup id="cite_ref-Intractability_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intractability-40"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Human beings solve most of their problems using fast, intuitive judgments rather than the conscious, step-by-step deduction that early AI research was able to model.<sup id="cite_ref-Psychological_evidence_of_sub-symbolic_reasoning_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Psychological_evidence_of_sub-symbolic_reasoning-41"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> AI has made some progress at imitating this kind of "sub-symbolic" problem solving: <a href="/wiki/Embodied_agent" title="Embodied agent">embodied agent</a> approaches emphasize the importance of <a href="/wiki/Sensorimotor" title="Sensorimotor">sensorimotor</a> skills to higher reasoning; <a href="/wiki/Neural_net" title="Neural net" class="mw-redirect">neural net</a> research attempts to simulate the structures inside human and animal brains that give rise to this skill.</p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Knowledge representation">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Knowledge_representation">Knowledge representation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GFO_taxonomy_tree.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/GFO_taxonomy_tree.png/220px-GFO_taxonomy_tree.png" width="220" height="208" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GFO_taxonomy_tree.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
An ontology represents knowledge as a set of concepts within a domain and the relationships between those concepts.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" title="Knowledge representation" class="mw-redirect">Knowledge representation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commonsense_knowledge" title="Commonsense knowledge" class="mw-redirect">Commonsense knowledge</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" title="Knowledge representation" class="mw-redirect">Knowledge representation</a><sup id="cite_ref-Knowledge_representation_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knowledge_representation-42"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_engineering" title="Knowledge engineering">knowledge engineering</a><sup id="cite_ref-Knowledge_engineering_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knowledge_engineering-43"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> are central to AI research. Many of the problems machines are expected to solve will require extensive knowledge about the world. Among the things that AI needs to represent are: objects, properties, categories and relations between objects;<sup id="cite_ref-Representing_categories_and_relations_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_categories_and_relations-44"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> situations, events, states and time;<sup id="cite_ref-Representing_time_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_time-45"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> causes and effects;<sup id="cite_ref-Representing_causation_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_causation-46"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> knowledge about knowledge (what we know about what other people know);<sup id="cite_ref-Representing_knowledge_about_knowledge_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_knowledge_about_knowledge-47"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> and many other, less well researched domains. A representation of "what exists" is an <a href="/wiki/Ontology_(computer_science)" title="Ontology (computer science)" class="mw-redirect">ontology</a> (borrowing a word from traditional <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>), of which the most general are called <a href="/wiki/Upper_ontology" title="Upper ontology" class="mw-redirect">upper ontologies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ontology_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ontology-48"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Among the most difficult problems in knowledge representation are:</p>
<dl>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Default_reasoning" title="Default reasoning" class="mw-redirect">Default reasoning</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Qualification_problem" title="Qualification problem">qualification problem</a></dt>
<dd>Many of the things people know take the form of "working assumptions." For example, if a bird comes up in conversation, people typically picture an animal that is fist sized, sings, and flies. None of these things are true about all birds. <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)">John McCarthy</a> identified this problem in 1969<sup id="cite_ref-Qualification_problem_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Qualification_problem-49"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> as the qualification problem: for any commonsense rule that AI researchers care to represent, there tend to be a huge number of exceptions. Almost nothing is simply true or false in the way that abstract logic requires. AI research has explored a number of solutions to this problem.<sup id="cite_ref-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic-50"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>The breadth of <a href="/wiki/Commonsense_knowledge" title="Commonsense knowledge" class="mw-redirect">commonsense knowledge</a></dt>
<dd>The number of atomic facts that the average person knows is astronomical. Research projects that attempt to build a complete knowledge base of <a href="/wiki/Commonsense_knowledge" title="Commonsense knowledge" class="mw-redirect">commonsense knowledge</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Cyc" title="Cyc">Cyc</a>) require enormous amounts of laborious <a href="/wiki/Ontology_engineering" title="Ontology engineering">ontological engineering</a> — they must be built, by hand, one complicated concept at a time.<sup id="cite_ref-Breadth_of_commonsense_knowledge_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Breadth_of_commonsense_knowledge-51"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> A major goal is to have the computer understand enough concepts to be able to learn by reading from sources like the internet, and thus be able to add to its own ontology.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources from October 2010">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>The subsymbolic form of some <a href="/wiki/Commonsense_knowledge" title="Commonsense knowledge" class="mw-redirect">commonsense knowledge</a></dt>
<dd>Much of what people know is not represented as "facts" or "statements" that they could express verbally. For example, a chess master will avoid a particular chess position because it "feels too exposed"<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> or an art critic can take one look at a statue and instantly realize that it is a fake.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> These are intuitions or tendencies that are represented in the brain non-consciously and sub-symbolically.<sup id="cite_ref-Intuition_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intuition-54"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Knowledge like this informs, supports and provides a context for symbolic, conscious knowledge. As with the related problem of sub-symbolic reasoning, it is hoped that <a href="/wiki/Situated_artificial_intelligence" title="Situated artificial intelligence" class="mw-redirect">situated AI</a> or <a href="/wiki/Computational_intelligence" title="Computational intelligence">computational intelligence</a> will provide ways to represent this kind of knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-Intuition_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intuition-54"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Planning">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Planning">Planning</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hierarchical-control-system.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/76/Hierarchical-control-system.svg/220px-Hierarchical-control-system.svg.png" width="220" height="199" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hierarchical-control-system.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Hierarchical_control_system" title="Hierarchical control system">hierarchical control system</a> is a form of <a href="/wiki/Control_system" title="Control system">control system</a> in which a set of devices and governing software is arranged in a hierarchy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">Automated planning and scheduling</a></div>
<p>Intelligent agents must be able to set goals and achieve them.<sup id="cite_ref-Planning_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Planning-55"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> They need a way to visualize the future (they must have a representation of the state of the world and be able to make predictions about how their actions will change it) and be able to make choices that maximize the <a href="/wiki/Utility" title="Utility">utility</a> (or "value") of the available choices.<sup id="cite_ref-Information_value_theory_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Information_value_theory-56"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In classical planning problems, the agent can assume that it is the only thing acting on the world and it can be certain what the consequences of its actions may be.<sup id="cite_ref-Classical_planning_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Classical_planning-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> However, if this is not true, it must periodically check if the world matches its predictions and it must change its plan as this becomes necessary, requiring the agent to reason under uncertainty.<sup id="cite_ref-Non-deterministic_planning_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Non-deterministic_planning-58"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Multi-agent_planning" title="Multi-agent planning">Multi-agent planning</a> uses the <a href="/wiki/Cooperation" title="Cooperation">cooperation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Competition" title="Competition">competition</a> of many agents to achieve a given goal. <a href="/wiki/Emergent_behavior" title="Emergent behavior" class="mw-redirect">Emergent behavior</a> such as this is used by <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_algorithms" title="Evolutionary algorithms" class="mw-redirect">evolutionary algorithms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swarm_intelligence" title="Swarm intelligence">swarm intelligence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Multi-agent_planning_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Multi-agent_planning-59"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Learning">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Learning">Learning</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">Machine learning</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">Machine learning</a><sup id="cite_ref-Machine_learning_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Machine_learning-60"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> has been central to AI research from the beginning.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1956, at the original Dartmouth AI summer conference, <a href="/wiki/Ray_Solomonoff" title="Ray Solomonoff">Ray Solomonoff</a> wrote a report on unsupervised probabilistic machine learning: "An Inductive Inference Machine".<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Unsupervised_learning" title="Unsupervised learning">Unsupervised learning</a> is the ability to find patterns in a stream of input. <a href="/wiki/Supervised_learning" title="Supervised learning">Supervised learning</a> includes both <a href="/wiki/Statistical_classification" title="Statistical classification">classification</a> and numerical <a href="/wiki/Regression_analysis" title="Regression analysis">regression</a>. Classification is used to determine what category something belongs in, after seeing a number of examples of things from several categories. Regression is the attempt to produce a function that describes the relationship between inputs and outputs and predicts how the outputs should change as the inputs change. In <a href="/wiki/Reinforcement_learning" title="Reinforcement learning">reinforcement learning</a><sup id="cite_ref-Reinforcement_learning_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reinforcement_learning-63"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> the agent is rewarded for good responses and punished for bad ones. These can be analyzed in terms of <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">decision theory</a>, using concepts like <a href="/wiki/Utility_(economics)" title="Utility (economics)" class="mw-redirect">utility</a>. The mathematical analysis of machine learning algorithms and their performance is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_computer_science" title="Theoretical computer science">theoretical computer science</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Computational_learning_theory" title="Computational learning theory">computational learning theory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Computational_learning_theory_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Computational_learning_theory-64"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Natural language processing">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_language_processing">Natural language processing</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ParseTree.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/ParseTree.svg/220px-ParseTree.svg.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ParseTree.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Parse_tree" title="Parse tree">parse tree</a> represents the <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntactic</a> structure of a sentence according to some <a href="/wiki/Formal_grammar" title="Formal grammar">formal grammar</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_processing" title="Natural language processing">Natural language processing</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Natural_language_processing" title="Natural language processing">Natural language processing</a><sup id="cite_ref-Natural_language_processing_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_language_processing-65"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> gives machines the ability to read and understand the languages that humans speak. A sufficiently powerful natural language processing system would enable <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_user_interface" title="Natural language user interface">natural language user interfaces</a> and the acquisition of knowledge directly from human-written sources, such as Internet texts. Some straightforward applications of natural language processing include <a href="/wiki/Information_retrieval" title="Information retrieval">information retrieval</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Text_mining" title="Text mining">text mining</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Machine_translation" title="Machine translation">machine translation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Applications_of_natural_language_processing_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Applications_of_natural_language_processing-66"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup>.</p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Motion and manipulation">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Motion_and_manipulation">Motion and manipulation</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">Robotics</a></div>
<p>The field of <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a><sup id="cite_ref-Robotics_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robotics-67"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> is closely related to AI. Intelligence is required for robots to be able to handle such tasks as object manipulation<sup id="cite_ref-Configuration_space_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Configuration_space-68"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Motion_planning" title="Motion planning">navigation</a>, with sub-problems of <a href="/wiki/Robot_localization" title="Robot localization" class="mw-redirect">localization</a> (knowing where you are), <a href="/wiki/Robotic_mapping" title="Robotic mapping">mapping</a> (learning what is around you) and <a href="/wiki/Motion_planning" title="Motion planning">motion planning</a> (figuring out how to get there).<sup id="cite_ref-Robotic_mapping_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robotic_mapping-69"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Perception">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Perception">Perception</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Machine_perception" title="Machine perception">Machine perception</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_vision" title="Computer vision">Computer vision</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Speech_recognition" title="Speech recognition">Speech recognition</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Machine_perception" title="Machine perception">Machine perception</a><sup id="cite_ref-Machine_perception_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Machine_perception-70"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> is the ability to use input from sensors (such as cameras, microphones, sonar and others more exotic) to deduce aspects of the world. <a href="/wiki/Computer_vision" title="Computer vision">Computer vision</a><sup id="cite_ref-Computer_vision_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Computer_vision-71"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> is the ability to analyze visual input. A few selected subproblems are <a href="/wiki/Speech_recognition" title="Speech recognition">speech recognition</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Speech_recognition_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speech_recognition-72"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Facial_recognition_system" title="Facial recognition system">facial recognition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Object_recognition" title="Object recognition" class="mw-redirect">object recognition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Object_recognition_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Object_recognition-73"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Social intelligence">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Social_intelligence">Social intelligence</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Affective_computing" title="Affective computing">Affective computing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kismet_robot_at_MIT_Museum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Kismet_robot_at_MIT_Museum.jpg/220px-Kismet_robot_at_MIT_Museum.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kismet_robot_at_MIT_Museum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Kismet_(robot)" title="Kismet (robot)">Kismet</a>, a robot with rudimentary social skills</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources from November 2011">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Emotion and social skills<sup id="cite_ref-Emotion_and_affective_computing_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emotion_and_affective_computing-74"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> play two roles for an intelligent agent. First, it must be able to predict the actions of others, by understanding their motives and emotional states. (This involves elements of <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">game theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">decision theory</a>, as well as the ability to model human emotions and the perceptual skills to detect emotions.) Also, in an effort to facilitate <a href="/wiki/Human-computer_interaction" title="Human-computer interaction" class="mw-redirect">human-computer interaction</a>, an intelligent machine might want to be able to <i>display</i> emotions—even if it does not actually experience them itself—in order to appear sensitive to the emotional dynamics of human interaction.</p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Creativity">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Creativity">Creativity</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Computational_creativity" title="Computational creativity">Computational creativity</a></div>
<p>A sub-field of AI addresses <a href="/wiki/Creativity" title="Creativity">creativity</a> both theoretically (from a philosophical and psychological perspective) and practically (via specific implementations of systems that generate outputs that can be considered creative, or systems that identify and assess creativity). Related areas of computational research are <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intuition" title="Artificial intuition">Artificial intuition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artificial_imagination" title="Artificial imagination">Artificial imagination</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources from January 2011">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: General intelligence">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="General_intelligence">General intelligence</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Strong_AI" title="Strong AI">Strong AI</a> and <a href="/wiki/AI-complete" title="AI-complete">AI-complete</a></div>
<p>Most researchers hope that their work will eventually be incorporated into a machine with <i>general</i> intelligence (known as <a href="/wiki/Strong_AI" title="Strong AI">strong AI</a>), combining all the skills above and exceeding human abilities at most or all of them.<sup id="cite_ref-General_intelligence_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-General_intelligence-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> A few believe that <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphic" title="Anthropomorphic" class="mw-redirect">anthropomorphic</a> features like <a href="/wiki/Artificial_consciousness" title="Artificial consciousness">artificial consciousness</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Artificial_brain" title="Artificial brain">artificial brain</a> may be required for such a project.<sup id="cite_ref-Artificial_consciousness_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Artificial_consciousness-75"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brain_simulation_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brain_simulation-76"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many of the problems above are considered <a href="/wiki/AI-complete" title="AI-complete">AI-complete</a>: to solve one problem, you must solve them all. For example, even a straightforward, specific task like <a href="/wiki/Machine_translation" title="Machine translation">machine translation</a> requires that the machine follow the author's argument (<a href="#Deduction.2C_reasoning.2C_problem_solving">reason</a>), know what is being talked about (<a href="#Knowledge_representation">knowledge</a>), and faithfully reproduce the author's intention (<a href="#Social_intelligence">social intelligence</a>). <a href="/wiki/Machine_translation" title="Machine translation">Machine translation</a>, therefore, is believed to be AI-complete: it may require <a href="/wiki/Strong_AI" title="Strong AI">strong AI</a> to be done as well as humans can do it.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_complete_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_complete-77"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Approaches">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Approaches">Approaches</span></h2>
<p>There is no established unifying theory or <a href="/wiki/Paradigm" title="Paradigm">paradigm</a> that guides AI research. Researchers disagree about many issues.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> A few of the most long standing questions that have remained unanswered are these: should artificial intelligence simulate natural intelligence by studying <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurology</a>? Or is human biology as irrelevant to AI research as bird biology is to <a href="/wiki/Aeronautical_engineering" title="Aeronautical engineering" class="mw-redirect">aeronautical engineering</a>?<sup id="cite_ref-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general-79"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Can intelligent behavior be described using simple, elegant principles (such as <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Optimization_(mathematics)" title="Optimization (mathematics)" class="mw-redirect">optimization</a>)? Or does it necessarily require solving a large number of completely unrelated problems?<sup id="cite_ref-Neats_vs._scruffies_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neats_vs._scruffies-80"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Can intelligence be reproduced using high-level symbols, similar to words and ideas? Or does it require "sub-symbolic" processing?<sup id="cite_ref-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic-81"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> John Haugeland, who coined the term GOFAI (Good Old-Fashioned Artificial Intelligence), also proposed that AI should more properly be referred to as <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_intelligence" title="Synthetic intelligence">synthetic intelligence</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaugeland1985255_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaugeland1985255-82"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> a term which has since been adopted by some non-GOFAI researchers.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wang2008_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wang2008-84"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cybernetics and brain simulation">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Cybernetics_and_brain_simulation">Cybernetics and brain simulation</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">Cybernetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computational_neuroscience" title="Computational neuroscience">Computational neuroscience</a></div>
<p>In the 1940s and 1950s, a number of researchers explored the connection between <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">cybernetics</a>. Some of them built machines that used electronic networks to exhibit rudimentary intelligence, such as <a href="/wiki/W._Grey_Walter" title="W. Grey Walter" class="mw-redirect">W. Grey Walter</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Turtle_(robot)" title="Turtle (robot)">turtles</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_Beast" title="Johns Hopkins Beast">Johns Hopkins Beast</a>. Many of these researchers gathered for meetings of the Teleological Society at <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ratio_Club" title="Ratio Club">Ratio Club</a> in England.<sup id="cite_ref-AI.27s_immediate_precursors_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI.27s_immediate_precursors-19"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> By 1960, this approach was largely abandoned, although elements of it would be revived in the 1980s.</p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Symbolic">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolic">Symbolic</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/GOFAI" title="GOFAI">GOFAI</a></div>
<p>When access to digital computers became possible in the middle 1950s, AI research began to explore the possibility that human intelligence could be reduced to symbol manipulation. The research was centered in three institutions: <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">CMU</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stanford" title="Stanford" class="mw-redirect">Stanford</a> and <a href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT" class="mw-redirect">MIT</a>, and each one developed its own style of research. <a href="/wiki/John_Haugeland" title="John Haugeland">John Haugeland</a> named these approaches to AI "good old fashioned AI" or "<a href="/wiki/GOFAI" title="GOFAI">GOFAI</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-GOFAI_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GOFAI-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Cognitive simulation</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Economist" title="Economist">Economist</a> <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Simon" title="Herbert Simon">Herbert Simon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allen_Newell" title="Allen Newell">Allen Newell</a> studied human problem-solving skills and attempted to formalize them, and their work laid the foundations of the field of artificial intelligence, as well as <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operations_research" title="Operations research">operations research</a> and <a href="/wiki/Management_science" title="Management science" class="mw-redirect">management science</a>. Their research team used the results of <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychological</a> experiments to develop programs that simulated the techniques that people used to solve problems. This tradition, centered at <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">Carnegie Mellon University</a> would eventually culminate in the development of the <a href="/wiki/Soar_(cognitive_architecture)" title="Soar (cognitive architecture)">Soar</a> architecture in the middle 80s.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_at_CMU_in_the_60s_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_at_CMU_in_the_60s-86"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Soar_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soar-87"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Logic-based</dt>
<dd>Unlike <a href="/wiki/Allen_Newell" title="Allen Newell">Newell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Simon" title="Herbert Simon">Simon</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)">John McCarthy</a> felt that machines did not need to simulate human thought, but should instead try to find the essence of abstract reasoning and problem solving, regardless of whether people used the same algorithms.<sup id="cite_ref-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general-79"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> His laboratory at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford</a> (<a href="/wiki/Stanford_Artificial_Intelligence_Laboratory" title="Stanford Artificial Intelligence Laboratory" class="mw-redirect">SAIL</a>) focused on using formal <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> to solve a wide variety of problems, including <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" title="Knowledge representation" class="mw-redirect">knowledge representation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">planning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_at_Stanford_in_the_60s_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_at_Stanford_in_the_60s-88"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> Logic was also focus of the work at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a> and elsewhere in Europe which led to the development of the programming language <a href="/wiki/Prolog" title="Prolog">Prolog</a> and the science of <a href="/wiki/Logic_programming" title="Logic programming">logic programming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_at_Edinburgh_and_France_in_the_60s_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_at_Edinburgh_and_France_in_the_60s-89"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>"Anti-logic" or "scruffy"</dt>
<dd>Researchers at <a href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT" class="mw-redirect">MIT</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Minsky" title="Marvin Minsky">Marvin Minsky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Papert" title="Seymour Papert">Seymour Papert</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-AI_at_MIT_in_the_60s_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_at_MIT_in_the_60s-90"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> found that solving difficult problems in <a href="/wiki/Computer_vision" title="Computer vision">vision</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_processing" title="Natural language processing">natural language processing</a> required ad-hoc solutions – they argued that there was no simple and general principle (like <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>) that would capture all the aspects of intelligent behavior. <a href="/wiki/Roger_Schank" title="Roger Schank">Roger Schank</a> described their "anti-logic" approaches as "<a href="/wiki/Neats_vs._scruffies" title="Neats vs. scruffies">scruffy</a>" (as opposed to the "<a href="/wiki/Neats_vs._scruffies" title="Neats vs. scruffies">neat</a>" paradigms at <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">CMU</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stanford" title="Stanford" class="mw-redirect">Stanford</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Neats_vs._scruffies_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neats_vs._scruffies-80"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Commonsense_knowledge_bases" title="Commonsense knowledge bases" class="mw-redirect">Commonsense knowledge bases</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Doug_Lenat" title="Doug Lenat" class="mw-redirect">Doug Lenat</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Cyc" title="Cyc">Cyc</a>) are an example of "scruffy" AI, since they must be built by hand, one complicated concept at a time.<sup id="cite_ref-Cyc_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cyc-91"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Knowledge-based</dt>
<dd>When computers with large memories became available around 1970, researchers from all three traditions began to build <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" title="Knowledge representation" class="mw-redirect">knowledge</a> into AI applications.<sup id="cite_ref-Knowledge_revolution_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knowledge_revolution-92"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> This "knowledge revolution" led to the development and deployment of <a href="/wiki/Expert_system" title="Expert system">expert systems</a> (introduced by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Feigenbaum" title="Edward Feigenbaum">Edward Feigenbaum</a>), the first truly successful form of AI software.<sup id="cite_ref-Expert_systems_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Expert_systems-29"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> The knowledge revolution was also driven by the realization that enormous amounts of knowledge would be required by many simple AI applications.</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Sub-symbolic">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-symbolic">Sub-symbolic</span></h3>
<p>During the 1960s, symbolic approaches had achieved great success at simulating high-level thinking in small demonstration programs. Approaches based on <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">cybernetics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neural_network" title="Neural network">neural networks</a> were abandoned or pushed into the background.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> By the 1980s, however, progress in symbolic AI seemed to stall and many believed that symbolic systems would never be able to imitate all the processes of human cognition, especially <a href="/wiki/Machine_perception" title="Machine perception">perception</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pattern_recognition" title="Pattern recognition">pattern recognition</a>. A number of researchers began to look into "sub-symbolic" approaches to specific AI problems.<sup id="cite_ref-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic-81"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Bottom-up, <a href="/wiki/Embodied_agent" title="Embodied agent">embodied</a>, <a href="/wiki/Situated" title="Situated">situated</a>, <a href="/wiki/Behavior-based_AI" title="Behavior-based AI" class="mw-redirect">behavior-based</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nouvelle_AI" title="Nouvelle AI">nouvelle AI</a></dt>
<dd>Researchers from the related field of <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Brooks" title="Rodney Brooks">Rodney Brooks</a>, rejected symbolic AI and focused on the basic engineering problems that would allow robots to move and survive.<sup id="cite_ref-Embodied_AI_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Embodied_AI-94"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Their work revived the non-symbolic viewpoint of the early <a href="/wiki/Cybernetic" title="Cybernetic" class="mw-redirect">cybernetics</a> researchers of the 50s and reintroduced the use of <a href="/wiki/Control_theory" title="Control theory">control theory</a> in AI. This coincided with the development of the <a href="/wiki/Embodied_mind_thesis" title="Embodied mind thesis" class="mw-redirect">embodied mind thesis</a> in the related field of <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>: the idea that aspects of the body (such as movement, perception and visualization) are required for higher intelligence.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Computational Intelligence</dt>
<dd>Interest in <a href="/wiki/Neural_networks" title="Neural networks" class="mw-redirect">neural networks</a> and "<a href="/wiki/Connectionism" title="Connectionism">connectionism</a>" was revived by <a href="/wiki/David_Rumelhart" title="David Rumelhart">David Rumelhart</a> and others in the middle 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-Revival_of_connectionism_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Revival_of_connectionism-95"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> These and other sub-symbolic approaches, such as <a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_system" title="Fuzzy system" class="mw-redirect">fuzzy systems</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_computation" title="Evolutionary computation">evolutionary computation</a>, are now studied collectively by the emerging discipline of <a href="/wiki/Computational_intelligence" title="Computational intelligence">computational intelligence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Computational_intelligence_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Computational_intelligence-96"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Statistical">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Statistical">Statistical</span></h3>
<p>In the 1990s, AI researchers developed sophisticated mathematical tools to solve specific subproblems. These tools are truly <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific</a>, in the sense that their results are both measurable and verifiable, and they have been responsible for many of AI's recent successes. The shared mathematical language has also permitted a high level of collaboration with more established fields (like <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Operations_research" title="Operations research">operations research</a>). <a href="/wiki/Stuart_J._Russell" title="Stuart J. Russell">Stuart Russell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Norvig" title="Peter Norvig">Peter Norvig</a> describe this movement as nothing less than a "revolution" and "the victory of the <a href="/wiki/Neats_and_scruffies" title="Neats and scruffies" class="mw-redirect">neats</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-Formal_methods_in_AI_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Formal_methods_in_AI-32"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> Critiques argue that these techniques are too focussed on particular problems and have failed to address the long term goal of general intelligence. <sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Integrating the approaches">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Integrating_the_approaches">Integrating the approaches</span></h3>
<dl>
<dt>Intelligent agent paradigm</dt>
<dd>An <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_agent" title="Intelligent agent">intelligent agent</a> is a system that perceives its environment and takes actions which maximize its chances of success. The simplest intelligent agents are programs that solve specific problems. More complicated agents include human beings and organizations of human beings (such as <a href="/wiki/Firm" title="Firm" class="mw-redirect">firms</a>). The paradigm gives researchers license to study isolated problems and find solutions that are both verifiable and useful, without agreeing on one single approach. An agent that solves a specific problem can use any approach that works — some agents are symbolic and logical, some are sub-symbolic <a href="/wiki/Neural_network" title="Neural network">neural networks</a> and others may use new approaches. The paradigm also gives researchers a common language to communicate with other fields—such as <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">decision theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>—that also use concepts of abstract agents. The intelligent agent paradigm became widely accepted during the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-Intelligent_agents_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intelligent_agents-1"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Agent_architecture" title="Agent architecture">Agent architectures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_architecture" title="Cognitive architecture">cognitive architectures</a></dt>
<dd>Researchers have designed systems to build intelligent systems out of interacting <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_agents" title="Intelligent agents" class="mw-redirect">intelligent agents</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Multi-agent_system" title="Multi-agent system">multi-agent system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Agent_architectures_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agent_architectures-98"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> A system with both symbolic and sub-symbolic components is a <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_intelligent_system" title="Hybrid intelligent system">hybrid intelligent system</a>, and the study of such systems is <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_systems_integration" title="Artificial intelligence systems integration">artificial intelligence systems integration</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Hierarchical_control_system" title="Hierarchical control system">hierarchical control system</a> provides a bridge between sub-symbolic AI at its lowest, reactive levels and traditional symbolic AI at its highest levels, where relaxed time constraints permit planning and world modelling.<sup id="cite_ref-Hierarchical_control_system_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hierarchical_control_system-99"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Brooks" title="Rodney Brooks">Rodney Brooks</a>' <a href="/wiki/Subsumption_architecture" title="Subsumption architecture">subsumption architecture</a> was an early proposal for such a hierarchical system.<sup id="cite_ref-Subsumption_architecture_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Subsumption_architecture-100"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Tools">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Tools">Tools</span></h2>
<p>In the course of 50 years of research, AI has developed a large number of tools to solve the most difficult problems in <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>. A few of the most general of these methods are discussed below.</p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Search and optimization">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Search_and_optimization">Search and optimization</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Search_algorithm" title="Search algorithm">Search algorithm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_optimization" title="Mathematical optimization">Mathematical optimization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_computation" title="Evolutionary computation">Evolutionary computation</a></div>
<p>Many problems in AI can be solved in theory by intelligently searching through many possible solutions:<sup id="cite_ref-Search_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Search-101"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="#Deduction.2C_reasoning.2C_problem_solving">Reasoning</a> can be reduced to performing a search. For example, logical proof can be viewed as searching for a path that leads from <a href="/wiki/Premise" title="Premise">premises</a> to <a href="/wiki/Logical_consequence" title="Logical consequence" class="mw-redirect">conclusions</a>, where each step is the application of an <a href="/wiki/Inference_rule" title="Inference rule" class="mw-redirect">inference rule</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Logic_as_search_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Logic_as_search-102"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">Planning</a> algorithms search through trees of goals and subgoals, attempting to find a path to a target goal, a process called <a href="/wiki/Means-ends_analysis" title="Means-ends analysis">means-ends analysis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Planning_as_search_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Planning_as_search-103"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">Robotics</a> algorithms for moving limbs and grasping objects use <a href="/wiki/Local_search_(optimization)" title="Local search (optimization)">local searches</a> in <a href="/wiki/Configuration_space" title="Configuration space">configuration space</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Configuration_space_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Configuration_space-68"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a> algorithms use search algorithms based on <a href="/wiki/Optimization_(mathematics)" title="Optimization (mathematics)" class="mw-redirect">optimization</a>.</p>
<p>Simple exhaustive searches<sup id="cite_ref-Uninformed_search_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uninformed_search-104"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> are rarely sufficient for most real world problems: the <a href="/wiki/Search_algorithm" title="Search algorithm">search space</a> (the number of places to search) quickly grows to <a href="/wiki/Astronomical" title="Astronomical" class="mw-redirect">astronomical</a> numbers. The result is a search that is <a href="/wiki/Computation_time" title="Computation time" class="mw-redirect">too slow</a> or never completes. The solution, for many problems, is to use "<a href="/wiki/Heuristics" title="Heuristics" class="mw-redirect">heuristics</a>" or "rules of thumb" that eliminate choices that are unlikely to lead to the goal (called "<a href="/wiki/Pruning_(algorithm)" title="Pruning (algorithm)" class="mw-redirect">pruning</a> the <a href="/wiki/Search_tree" title="Search tree">search tree</a>"). <a href="/wiki/Heuristics" title="Heuristics" class="mw-redirect">Heuristics</a> supply the program with a "best guess" for the path on which the solution lies.<sup id="cite_ref-Informed_search_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Informed_search-105"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A very different kind of search came to prominence in the 1990s, based on the mathematical theory of <a href="/wiki/Optimization_(mathematics)" title="Optimization (mathematics)" class="mw-redirect">optimization</a>. For many problems, it is possible to begin the search with some form of a guess and then refine the guess incrementally until no more refinements can be made. These algorithms can be visualized as blind <a href="/wiki/Hill_climbing" title="Hill climbing">hill climbing</a>: we begin the search at a random point on the landscape, and then, by jumps or steps, we keep moving our guess uphill, until we reach the top. Other optimization algorithms are <a href="/wiki/Simulated_annealing" title="Simulated annealing">simulated annealing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beam_search" title="Beam search">beam search</a> and <a href="/wiki/Random_optimization" title="Random optimization">random optimization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Optimization_search_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Optimization_search-106"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_computation" title="Evolutionary computation">Evolutionary computation</a> uses a form of optimization search. For example, they may begin with a population of organisms (the guesses) and then allow them to mutate and recombine, <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">selecting</a> only the fittest to survive each generation (refining the guesses). Forms of <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_computation" title="Evolutionary computation">evolutionary computation</a> include <a href="/wiki/Swarm_intelligence" title="Swarm intelligence">swarm intelligence</a> algorithms (such as <a href="/wiki/Ant_colony_optimization" title="Ant colony optimization" class="mw-redirect">ant colony</a> or <a href="/wiki/Particle_swarm_optimization" title="Particle swarm optimization">particle swarm optimization</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-Society_based_learning_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Society_based_learning-107"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_algorithms" title="Evolutionary algorithms" class="mw-redirect">evolutionary algorithms</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Genetic_algorithms" title="Genetic algorithms" class="mw-redirect">genetic algorithms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_programming" title="Genetic programming">genetic programming</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Genetic_programming_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetic_programming-108"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Logic">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Logic">Logic</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Logic_programming" title="Logic programming">Logic programming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Automated_reasoning" title="Automated reasoning">Automated reasoning</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">Logic</a><sup id="cite_ref-Logic_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Logic-109"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> is used for knowledge representation and problem solving, but it can be applied to other problems as well. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Satplan" title="Satplan">satplan</a> algorithm uses logic for <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">planning</a><sup id="cite_ref-Satplan_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Satplan-110"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Inductive_logic_programming" title="Inductive logic programming">inductive logic programming</a> is a method for <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Symbolic_learning_techniques_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Symbolic_learning_techniques-111"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Several different forms of logic are used in AI research. <a href="/wiki/Propositional_logic" title="Propositional logic" class="mw-redirect">Propositional</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sentential_logic" title="Sentential logic" class="mw-redirect">sentential logic</a><sup id="cite_ref-Propositional_logic_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Propositional_logic-112"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> is the logic of statements which can be true or false. <a href="/wiki/First-order_logic" title="First-order logic">First-order logic</a><sup id="cite_ref-First-order_logic_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-First-order_logic-113"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> also allows the use of <a href="/wiki/Quantifier" title="Quantifier" class="mw-redirect">quantifiers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Predicate_(mathematical_logic)" title="Predicate (mathematical logic)">predicates</a>, and can express facts about objects, their properties, and their relations with each other. <a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_logic" title="Fuzzy logic">Fuzzy logic</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Fuzzy_logic_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuzzy_logic-114"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> is a version of first-order logic which allows the truth of a statement to be represented as a value between 0 and 1, rather
