than simply True (1) or False (0). <a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_system" title="Fuzzy system" class="mw-redirect">Fuzzy systems</a> can be used for uncertain reasoning and have been widely used in modern industrial and consumer product control systems. <a href="/wiki/Subjective_logic" title="Subjective logic">Subjective logic</a><sup id="cite_ref-Subjective_logic_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Subjective_logic-115"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> models uncertainty in a different and more explicit manner than fuzzy-logic: a given binomial opinion satisfies belief + disbelief + uncertainty = 1 within a <a href="/wiki/Beta_distribution" title="Beta distribution">Beta distribution</a>. By this method, ignorance can be distinguished from probabilistic statements that an agent makes with high confidence.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Default_logic" title="Default logic">Default logics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-monotonic_logic" title="Non-monotonic logic">non-monotonic logics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Circumscription_(logic)" title="Circumscription (logic)">circumscription</a><sup id="cite_ref-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic-50"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> are forms of logic designed to help with default reasoning and the <a href="/wiki/Qualification_problem" title="Qualification problem">qualification problem</a>. Several extensions of logic have been designed to handle specific domains of <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" title="Knowledge representation" class="mw-redirect">knowledge</a>, such as: <a href="/wiki/Description_logic" title="Description logic">description logics</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Representing_categories_and_relations_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_categories_and_relations-44"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Situation_calculus" title="Situation calculus">situation calculus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Event_calculus" title="Event calculus">event calculus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fluent_calculus" title="Fluent calculus">fluent calculus</a> (for representing events and time);<sup id="cite_ref-Representing_time_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_time-45"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Causality#Causal_calculus" title="Causality">causal calculus</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Representing_causation_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_causation-46"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> belief calculus; and <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal logics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Representing_knowledge_about_knowledge_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Representing_knowledge_about_knowledge-47"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Probabilistic methods for uncertain reasoning">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Probabilistic_methods_for_uncertain_reasoning">Probabilistic methods for uncertain reasoning</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_network" title="Bayesian network">Bayesian network</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hidden_Markov_model" title="Hidden Markov model">Hidden Markov model</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalman_filter" title="Kalman filter">Kalman filter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">Decision theory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Utility_theory" title="Utility theory" class="mw-redirect">Utility theory</a></div>
<p>Many problems in AI (in reasoning, planning, learning, perception and robotics) require the agent to operate with incomplete or uncertain information. AI researchers have devised a number of powerful tools to solve these problems using methods from <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probability</a> theory and <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stochastic_methods_for_uncertain_reasoning_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stochastic_methods_for_uncertain_reasoning-116"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bayesian_network" title="Bayesian network">Bayesian networks</a><sup id="cite_ref-Bayesian_networks_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bayesian_networks-117"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> are a very general tool that can be used for a large number of problems: reasoning (using the <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_inference" title="Bayesian inference">Bayesian inference</a> algorithm),<sup id="cite_ref-Bayesian_inference_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bayesian_inference-118"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a> (using the <a href="/wiki/Expectation-maximization_algorithm" title="Expectation-maximization algorithm" class="mw-redirect">expectation-maximization algorithm</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-Bayesian_learning_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bayesian_learning-119"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling" title="Automated planning and scheduling">planning</a> (using <a href="/wiki/Decision_network" title="Decision network" class="mw-redirect">decision networks</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-Bayesian_decision_networks_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bayesian_decision_networks-120"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Machine_perception" title="Machine perception">perception</a> (using <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_Bayesian_network" title="Dynamic Bayesian network">dynamic Bayesian networks</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Stochastic_temporal_models_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stochastic_temporal_models-121"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Probabilistic algorithms can also be used for filtering, prediction, smoothing and finding explanations for streams of data, helping <a href="/wiki/Machine_perception" title="Machine perception">perception</a> systems to analyze processes that occur over time (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Hidden_Markov_model" title="Hidden Markov model">hidden Markov models</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kalman_filter" title="Kalman filter">Kalman filters</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Stochastic_temporal_models_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stochastic_temporal_models-121"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A key concept from the science of <a href="/wiki/Economic" title="Economic" class="mw-redirect">economics</a> is "<a href="/wiki/Utility" title="Utility">utility</a>": a measure of how valuable something is to an intelligent agent. Precise mathematical tools have been developed that analyze how an agent can make choices and plan, using <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">decision theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decision_analysis" title="Decision analysis">decision analysis</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Decisions_theory_and_analysis_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Decisions_theory_and_analysis-122"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Applied_information_economics" title="Applied information economics">information value theory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Information_value_theory_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Information_value_theory-56"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> These tools include models such as <a href="/wiki/Markov_decision_process" title="Markov decision process">Markov decision processes</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Markov_decision_process_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Markov_decision_process-123"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> dynamic <a href="/wiki/Decision_network" title="Decision network" class="mw-redirect">decision networks</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Stochastic_temporal_models_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stochastic_temporal_models-121"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">game theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_design" title="Mechanism design">mechanism design</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Game_theory_and_mechanism_design_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Game_theory_and_mechanism_design-124"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Classifiers and statistical learning methods">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Classifiers_and_statistical_learning_methods">Classifiers and statistical learning methods</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Classifier_(mathematics)" title="Classifier (mathematics)" class="mw-redirect">Classifier (mathematics)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Statistical_classification" title="Statistical classification">Statistical classification</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">Machine learning</a></div>
<p>The simplest AI applications can be divided into two types: classifiers ("if shiny then diamond") and controllers ("if shiny then pick up"). Controllers do however also classify conditions before inferring actions, and therefore classification forms a central part of many AI systems. <a href="/wiki/Classifier_(mathematics)" title="Classifier (mathematics)" class="mw-redirect">Classifiers</a> are functions that use <a href="/wiki/Pattern_matching" title="Pattern matching">pattern matching</a> to determine a closest match. They can be tuned according to examples, making them very attractive for use in AI. These examples are known as observations or patterns. In supervised learning, each pattern belongs to a certain predefined class. A class can be seen as a decision that has to be made. All the observations combined with their class labels are known as a data set. When a new observation is received, that observation is classified based on previous experience.<sup id="cite_ref-Classifiers_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Classifiers-125"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A classifier can be trained in various ways; there are many statistical and <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a> approaches. The most widely used classifiers are the <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">neural network</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Neural_networks_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neural_networks-126"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kernel_methods" title="Kernel methods">kernel methods</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Support_vector_machine" title="Support vector machine">support vector machine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Kernel_methods_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kernel_methods-127"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/K-nearest_neighbor_algorithm" title="K-nearest neighbor algorithm">k-nearest neighbor algorithm</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-K-nearest_neighbor_algorithm_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K-nearest_neighbor_algorithm-128"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_mixture_model" title="Gaussian mixture model" class="mw-redirect">Gaussian mixture model</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Guassian_mixture_model_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guassian_mixture_model-129"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Naive_Bayes_classifier" title="Naive Bayes classifier">naive Bayes classifier</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Naive_Bayes_classifier_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naive_Bayes_classifier-130"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Decision_tree_learning" title="Decision tree learning">decision tree</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Decision_tree_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Decision_tree-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> The performance of these classifiers have been compared over a wide range of tasks. Classifier performance depends greatly on the characteristics of the data to be classified. There is no single classifier that works best on all given problems; this is also referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/No_free_lunch_in_search_and_optimization" title="No free lunch in search and optimization">no free lunch</a>" theorem. Determining a suitable classifier for a given problem is still more an art than science.<sup id="cite_ref-Classifier_performance_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Classifier_performance-132"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Neural networks">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Neural_networks">Neural networks</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Neural_network" title="Neural network">Neural network</a> and <a href="/wiki/Connectionism" title="Connectionism">Connectionism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Artificial_neural_network.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Artificial_neural_network.svg/220px-Artificial_neural_network.svg.png" width="220" height="196" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Artificial_neural_network.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A neural network is an interconnected group of nodes, akin to the vast network of <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">human brain</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The study of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_neural_network" title="Artificial neural network">artificial neural networks</a><sup id="cite_ref-Neural_networks_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neural_networks-126"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> began in the decade before the field AI research was founded, in the work of <a href="/wiki/Walter_Pitts" title="Walter Pitts">Walter Pitts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warren_McCullough" title="Warren McCullough" class="mw-redirect">Warren McCullough</a>. Other important early researchers were <a href="/wiki/Frank_Rosenblatt" title="Frank Rosenblatt">Frank Rosenblatt</a>, who invented the <a href="/wiki/Perceptron" title="Perceptron">perceptron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Werbos" title="Paul Werbos">Paul Werbos</a> who developed the <a href="/wiki/Backpropagation" title="Backpropagation">backpropagation</a> algorithm.<sup id="cite_ref-Backpropagation_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Backpropagation-133"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The main categories of networks are acyclic or <a href="/wiki/Feedforward_neural_network" title="Feedforward neural network">feedforward neural networks</a> (where the signal passes in only one direction) and <a href="/wiki/Recurrent_neural_network" title="Recurrent neural network">recurrent neural networks</a> (which allow feedback). Among the most popular feedforward networks are <a href="/wiki/Perceptron" title="Perceptron">perceptrons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multi-layer_perceptron" title="Multi-layer perceptron" class="mw-redirect">multi-layer perceptrons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radial_basis_network" title="Radial basis network" class="mw-redirect">radial basis networks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Feedforward_neural_networks_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feedforward_neural_networks-134"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> Among recurrent networks, the most famous is the <a href="/wiki/Hopfield_net" title="Hopfield net" class="mw-redirect">Hopfield net</a>, a form of attractor network, which was first described by <a href="/wiki/John_Hopfield" title="John Hopfield">John Hopfield</a> in 1982.<sup id="cite_ref-Recurrent_neural_networks_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Recurrent_neural_networks-135"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> Neural networks can be applied to the problem of <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_control" title="Intelligent control">intelligent control</a> (for robotics) or <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">learning</a>, using such techniques as <a href="/wiki/Hebbian_learning" title="Hebbian learning" class="mw-redirect">Hebbian learning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Competitive_learning" title="Competitive learning">competitive learning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Learning_in_neural_networks_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Learning_in_neural_networks-136"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hierarchical_temporal_memory" title="Hierarchical temporal memory">Hierarchical temporal memory</a> is an approach that models some of the structural and algorithmic properties of the <a href="/wiki/Neocortex" title="Neocortex">neocortex</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hierarchical_temporal_memory_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hierarchical_temporal_memory-137"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Control theory">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Control_theory">Control theory</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_control" title="Intelligent control">Intelligent control</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Control_theory" title="Control theory">Control theory</a>, the grandchild of <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">cybernetics</a>, has many important applications, especially in <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Control_theory_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Control_theory-138"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_programming_languages_for_artificial_intelligence" title="List of programming languages for artificial intelligence">List of programming languages for artificial intelligence</a></div>
<p>AI researchers have developed several specialized languages for AI research, including <a href="/wiki/Lisp_programming_language" title="Lisp programming language" class="mw-redirect">Lisp</a><sup id="cite_ref-Lisp_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lisp-139"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Prolog" title="Prolog">Prolog</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Prolog_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prolog-140"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Evaluating progress">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Evaluating_progress">Evaluating progress</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Progress_in_artificial_intelligence" title="Progress in artificial intelligence">Progress in artificial intelligence</a></div>
<p>In 1950, Alan Turing proposed a general procedure to test the intelligence of an agent now known as the <a href="/wiki/Turing_test" title="Turing test">Turing test</a>. This procedure allows almost all the major problems of artificial intelligence to be tested. However, it is a very difficult challenge and at present all agents fail.<sup id="cite_ref-Turing_test_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turing_test-141"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Artificial intelligence can also be evaluated on specific problems such as small problems in chemistry, hand-writing recognition and game-playing. Such tests have been termed <a href="/wiki/Subject_matter_expert_Turing_test" title="Subject matter expert Turing test">subject matter expert Turing tests</a>. Smaller problems provide more achievable goals and there are an ever-increasing number of positive results.<sup id="cite_ref-Subject_matter_expert_Turing_test_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Subject_matter_expert_Turing_test-142"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The broad classes of outcome for an AI test are: (1) Optimal: it is not possible to perform better. (2) Strong super-human: performs better than all humans. (3) Super-human: performs better than most humans. (4) Sub-human: performs worse than most humans.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources from January 2011">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> For example, performance at <a href="/wiki/Draughts" title="Draughts">draughts</a> is optimal,<sup id="cite_ref-Game_AI_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Game_AI-143"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> performance at chess is super-human and nearing strong super-human (see <a href="/wiki/Computer_chess#Computers_versus_humans" title="Computer chess">Computer chess#Computers versus humans</a>) and performance at many everyday tasks (such as recognizing a face or crossing a room without bumping into something) is sub-human.</p>
<p>A quite different approach measures machine intelligence through tests which are developed from <i>mathematical</i> definitions of intelligence. Examples of these kinds of tests start in the late nineties devising intelligence tests using notions from <a href="/wiki/Kolmogorov_complexity" title="Kolmogorov complexity">Kolmogorov complexity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Data_compression" title="Data compression">data compression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mathematical_definitions_of_intelligence_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mathematical_definitions_of_intelligence-144"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> Two major advantages of mathematical definitions are their applicability to nonhuman intelligences and their absence of a requirement for human testers.</p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Automated_online_assistant.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Automated_online_assistant.png/220px-Automated_online_assistant.png" width="220" height="276" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Automated_online_assistant.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Automated_online_assistant" title="Automated online assistant">automated online assistant</a> providing customer service on a web page - one of many very primitive applications of artificial intelligence.</div>
</div>
</div>

<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Applications_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Applications of artificial intelligence">Applications of artificial intelligence</a></div>
<p>Artificial intelligence techniques are pervasive and are too numerous to list. Frequently, when a technique reaches mainstream use, it is no longer considered artificial intelligence; this phenomenon is described as the <a href="/wiki/AI_effect" title="AI effect">AI effect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Competitions and prizes">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Competitions_and_prizes">Competitions and prizes</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Competitions_and_prizes_in_artificial_intelligence" title="Competitions and prizes in artificial intelligence">Competitions and prizes in artificial intelligence</a></div>
<p>There are a number of competitions and prizes to promote research in artificial intelligence. The main areas promoted are: general machine intelligence, conversational behavior, data-mining, driverless cars, robot soccer and games.</p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Platforms">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Platforms">Platforms</span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Platform_(computing)" title="Platform (computing)" class="mw-redirect">platform</a> (or "<a href="/wiki/Computing_platform" title="Computing platform">computing platform</a>") is defined as "some sort of hardware architecture or software framework (including application frameworks), that allows software to run." As Rodney Brooks<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> pointed out many years ago, it is not just the artificial intelligence software that defines the AI features of the platform, but rather the actual platform itself that affects the AI that results, i.e., there needs to be work in AI problems on real-world platforms rather than in isolation.</p>
<p>A wide variety of platforms has allowed different aspects of AI to develop, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Expert_systems" title="Expert systems" class="mw-redirect">expert systems</a>, albeit <a href="/wiki/Personal_Computer" title="Personal Computer" class="mw-redirect">PC</a>-based but still an entire real-world system, to various robot platforms such as the widely available <a href="/wiki/Roomba" title="Roomba">Roomba</a> with open interface.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Philosophy of artificial intelligence">Philosophy of artificial intelligence</a></div>
<p>Artificial intelligence, by claiming to be able to recreate the capabilities of the human <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a>, is both a challenge and an inspiration for <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>. Are there limits to how intelligent machines can be? Is there an essential difference between human intelligence and artificial intelligence? Can a machine have a <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> and <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a>? A few of the most influential answers to these questions are given below.<sup id="cite_ref-Philosophy_of_AI_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Philosophy_of_AI-148"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Computing_Machinery_and_Intelligence" title="Computing Machinery and Intelligence">Turing's "polite convention"</a>: We need not decide if a machine can "think"; we need only decide if a machine can act as intelligently as a human being. This approach to the philosophical problems associated with artificial intelligence forms the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Turing_test" title="Turing test">Turing test</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Turing_test_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turing_test-141"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Conferences" title="Dartmouth Conferences">Dartmouth proposal</a>: "Every aspect of learning or any other feature of intelligence can be so precisely described that a machine can be made to simulate it." This conjecture was printed in the proposal for the <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Conferences" title="Dartmouth Conferences">Dartmouth Conference</a> of 1956, and represents the position of most working AI researchers.<sup id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_proposal_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_proposal-149"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Physical_symbol_system" title="Physical symbol system">Newell and Simon's physical symbol system hypothesis</a>: "A physical symbol system has the necessary and sufficient means of general intelligent action." Newell and Simon argue that intelligences consist of formal operations on symbols.<sup id="cite_ref-Physical_symbol_system_hypothesis_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Physical_symbol_system_hypothesis-150"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Dreyfus" title="Hubert Dreyfus">Hubert Dreyfus</a> argued that, on the contrary, human expertise depends on unconscious instinct rather than conscious symbol manipulation and on having a "feel" for the situation rather than explicit symbolic knowledge. (See <a href="/wiki/Dreyfus%27_critique_of_AI" title="Dreyfus' critique of AI" class="mw-redirect">Dreyfus' critique of AI</a>.)<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dreyfus.27_critique_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dreyfus.27_critique-152"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_incompleteness_theorem" title="Gödel's incompleteness theorem" class="mw-redirect">Gödel's incompleteness theorem</a>: A <a href="/wiki/Formal_system" title="Formal system">formal system</a> (such as a computer program) cannot prove all true statements.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roger_Penrose" title="Roger Penrose">Roger Penrose</a> is among those who claim that Gödel's theorem limits what machines can do. (See <i><a href="/wiki/The_Emperor%27s_New_Mind" title="The Emperor's New Mind">The Emperor's New Mind</a></i>.)<sup id="cite_ref-The_mathematical_objection_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_mathematical_objection-154"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Strong_AI_hypothesis" title="Strong AI hypothesis" class="mw-redirect">Searle's strong AI hypothesis</a>: "The appropriately programmed computer with the right inputs and outputs would thereby have a mind in exactly the same sense human beings have minds."<sup id="cite_ref-Searle.27s_strong_AI_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Searle.27s_strong_AI-155"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> John Searle counters this assertion with his <a href="/wiki/Chinese_room" title="Chinese room">Chinese room</a> argument, which asks us to look <i>inside</i> the computer and try to find where the "mind" might be.<sup id="cite_ref-Chinese_room_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chinese_room-156"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Artificial_brain" title="Artificial brain">artificial brain</a> argument: The brain can be simulated. <a href="/wiki/Hans_Moravec" title="Hans Moravec">Hans Moravec</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil" title="Ray Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a> and others have argued that it is technologically feasible to copy the brain directly into hardware and software, and that such a simulation will be essentially identical to the original.<sup id="cite_ref-Brain_simulation_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brain_simulation-76"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Predictions and ethics">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Predictions_and_ethics">Predictions and ethics</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_in_fiction" title="Artificial intelligence in fiction">Artificial intelligence in fiction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Ethics of artificial intelligence">Ethics of artificial intelligence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">Transhumanism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Technological_singularity" title="Technological singularity">Technological singularity</a></div>
<p>Artificial Intelligence is a common topic in both <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> and projections about the future of technology and society. The existence of an artificial intelligence that rivals human intelligence raises difficult ethical issues, and the potential power of the technology inspires both hopes and fears.</p>
<p>In fiction, Artificial Intelligence has appeared fulfilling many roles, including a servant (<a href="/wiki/R2D2" title="R2D2" class="mw-redirect">R2D2</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars" title="Star Wars">Star Wars</a></i>), a law enforcer (<a href="/wiki/K.I.T.T." title="K.I.T.T." class="mw-redirect">K.I.T.T.</a> "<a href="/wiki/Knight_Rider_(1982_TV_series)" title="Knight Rider (1982 TV series)">Knight Rider</a>"), a comrade (<a href="/wiki/Data_(Star_Trek)" title="Data (Star Trek)">Lt. Commander Data</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Next_Generation" title="Star Trek: The Next Generation">Star Trek: The Next Generation</a></i>), a conqueror/overlord (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Matrix" title="The Matrix">The Matrix</a></i>), a dictator (<i><a href="/wiki/With_Folded_Hands" title="With Folded Hands">With Folded Hands</a></i>), a benevolent provider/de facto ruler (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Culture" title="The Culture">The Culture</a></i>), an assassin (<i><a href="/wiki/Terminator_(series)" title="Terminator (series)" class="mw-redirect">Terminator</a></i>), a sentient race (<i><a href="/wiki/Battlestar_Galactica_(re-imagining)" title="Battlestar Galactica (re-imagining)" class="mw-redirect">Battlestar Galactica</a></i>/<a href="/wiki/Transformers" title="Transformers">Transformers</a>), an extension to human abilities (<i><a href="/wiki/Ghost_in_the_Shell" title="Ghost in the Shell">Ghost in the Shell</a></i>) and the savior of the human race (<a href="/wiki/R._Daneel_Olivaw" title="R. Daneel Olivaw">R. Daneel Olivaw</a> in the <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asimov%27s_Robot_Series&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Asimov's Robot Series (page does not exist)">Asimov's Robot Series</a></i>).</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mary_Shelley" title="Mary Shelley">Mary Shelley</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein">Frankenstein</a></i> considers a key issue in the <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_artificial_intelligence" title="Ethics of artificial intelligence">ethics of artificial intelligence</a>: if a machine can be created that has intelligence, could it also <i><a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">feel</a></i>? If it can feel, does it have the same rights as a human? The idea also appears in modern <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>, including the films <i><a href="/wiki/I_Robot_(film)" title="I Robot (film)" class="mw-redirect">I Robot</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Blade_Runner" title="Blade Runner">Blade Runner</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/A.I.:_Artificial_Intelligence" title="A.I.: Artificial Intelligence" class="mw-redirect">A.I.: Artificial Intelligence</a></i>, in which humanoid machines have the ability to feel human emotions. This issue, now known as "<a href="/wiki/Robot_rights" title="Robot rights" class="mw-redirect">robot rights</a>", is currently being considered by, for example, California's <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_the_Future" title="Institute for the Future">Institute for the Future</a>, although many critics believe that the discussion is premature.<sup id="cite_ref-Robot_rights_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robot_rights-157"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> The subject is profoundly discussed in the 2010 documentary film <i><a href="/wiki/Plug_%26_Pray" title="Plug &amp; Pray">Plug &amp; Pray</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Martin Ford, author of <i>The Lights in the Tunnel: Automation, Accelerating Technology and the Economy of the Future</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ford2009Lights_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ford2009Lights-159"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> and others argue that specialized artificial intelligence applications, robotics and other forms of automation will ultimately result in significant <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a> as machines begin to match and exceed the capability of workers to perform most routine and repetitive jobs. Ford predicts that many knowledge-based occupations—and in particular entry level jobs—will be increasingly susceptible to automation via expert systems, machine learning<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> and other AI-enhanced applications. AI-based applications may also be used to amplify the capabilities of low-wage offshore workers, making it more feasible to <a href="/wiki/Outsource" title="Outsource" class="mw-redirect">outsource</a> knowledge work.<sup id="cite_ref-Replaced_by_machines_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Replaced_by_machines-161"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Weizenbaum" title="Joseph Weizenbaum">Joseph Weizenbaum</a> wrote that AI applications can not, by definition, successfully simulate genuine human empathy and that the use of AI technology in fields such as <a href="/wiki/Customer_service" title="Customer service">customer service</a> or <a href="/wiki/Psychotherapy" title="Psychotherapy">psychotherapy</a><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> was deeply misguided. Weizenbaum was also bothered that AI researchers (and some philosophers) were willing to view the human mind as nothing more than a computer program (a position now known as <a href="/wiki/Computationalism" title="Computationalism" class="mw-redirect">computationalism</a>). To Weizenbaum these points suggest that AI research devalues human life.<sup id="cite_ref-Weizenbaum.27s_critique_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weizenbaum.27s_critique-163"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many futurists believe that artificial intelligence will ultimately transcend the limits of progress. <a href="/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil" title="Ray Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a> has used <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore's law">Moore's law</a> (which describes the relentless exponential improvement in digital technology) to calculate that <a href="/wiki/Desktop_computer" title="Desktop computer">desktop computers</a> will have the same processing power as human brains by the year 2029. He also predicts that by 2045 artificial intelligence will reach a point where it is able to improve <i>itself</i> at a rate that far exceeds anything conceivable in the past, a scenario that <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> writer <a href="/wiki/Vernor_Vinge" title="Vernor Vinge">Vernor Vinge</a> named the "<a href="/wiki/Technological_singularity" title="Technological singularity">singularity</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Singularity_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singularity-164"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Robot designer <a href="/wiki/Hans_Moravec" title="Hans Moravec">Hans Moravec</a>, cyberneticist <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Warwick" title="Kevin Warwick">Kevin Warwick</a> and inventor <a href="/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil" title="Ray Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a> have predicted that humans and machines will merge in the future into <a href="/wiki/Cyborg" title="Cyborg">cyborgs</a> that are more capable and powerful than either.<sup id="cite_ref-Transhumanism_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Transhumanism-165"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup> This idea, called <a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">transhumanism</a>, which has roots in <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ettinger" title="Robert Ettinger">Robert Ettinger</a>, has been illustrated in fiction as well, for example in the <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Ghost_in_the_Shell" title="Ghost in the Shell">Ghost in the Shell</a></i> and the science-fiction series <i><a href="/wiki/Dune_(novel)" title="Dune (novel)">Dune</a></i>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Edward_Fredkin" title="Edward Fredkin">Edward Fredkin</a> argues that "artificial intelligence is the next stage in evolution," an idea first proposed by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Butler_(novelist)" title="Samuel Butler (novelist)">Samuel Butler</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Darwin_among_the_Machines" title="Darwin among the Machines">Darwin among the Machines</a>" (1863), and expanded upon by <a href="/wiki/George_Dyson_(science_historian)" title="George Dyson (science historian)">George Dyson</a> in his book of the same name in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_as_evolution_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_as_evolution-166"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pamela_McCorduck" title="Pamela McCorduck">Pamela McCorduck</a> writes that all these scenarios are expressions of the ancient human desire to, as she calls it, "forge the gods".<sup id="cite_ref-McCorduck.27s_thesis_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCorduck.27s_thesis-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>
<div class="reflist references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Definition_of_AI-0"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Definition_of_AI_0-0">^</a></b> Definition of AI as the study of <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_agents" title="Intelligent agents" class="mw-redirect">intelligent agents</a>:
<ul>
<li><a href="#CITEREFPooleMackworthGoebel1998">Poole, Mackworth &amp; Goebel 1998</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://people.cs.ubc.ca/~poole/ci/ch1.pdf">p. 1</a>, which provides the version that is used in this article. Note that they use the term "computational intelligence" as a synonym for artificial intelligence.</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig (2003)</a> (who prefer the term "rational agent") and write "The whole-agent view is now widely accepted in the field" (<a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, p.&#160;55).</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFNilsson1998">Nilsson 1998</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Intelligent_agents-1">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Intelligent_agents_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Intelligent_agents_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> The <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_agent" title="Intelligent agent">intelligent agent</a> paradigm:
<ul>
<li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, pp.&#160;27, 32–58, 968–972</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFPooleMackworthGoebel1998">Poole, Mackworth &amp; Goebel 1998</a>, pp.&#160;7–21</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFLugerStubblefield2004">Luger &amp; Stubblefield 2004</a>, pp.&#160;235–240</li>
</ul>
The definition used in this article, in terms of goals, actions, perception and environment, is due to <a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig (2003)</a>. Other definitions also include knowledge and learning as additional criteria.</li>
<li id="cite_note-Coining_of_the_term_AI-2"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Coining_of_the_term_AI_2-0">^</a></b> Although there is some controversy on this point (see <a href="#CITEREFCrevier1993">Crevier (1993</a>, p.&#160;50)), <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)">McCarthy</a> states unequivocally "I came up with the term" in a c|net interview. (<a href="#CITEREFSkillings2006">Skillings 2006</a>)</li>
<li id="cite_note-McCarthy.27s_definition_of_AI-3"><b><a href="#cite_ref-McCarthy.27s_definition_of_AI_3-0">^</a></b> <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)">McCarthy</a>'s definition of AI:
<ul>
<li><a href="#CITEREFMcCarthy2007">McCarthy 2007</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b> See the <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_conference" title="Dartmouth conference" class="mw-redirect">Dartmouth proposal</a>, under <a href="#Philosophy">Philosophy</a>, below.</li>
<li id="cite_note-McCorduck.27s_thesis-5">^ <a href="#cite_ref-McCorduck.27s_thesis_5-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-McCorduck.27s_thesis_5-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-McCorduck.27s_thesis_5-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> This is a central idea of <a href="/wiki/Pamela_McCorduck" title="Pamela McCorduck">Pamela McCorduck</a>'s <i>Machines That Think</i>. She writes: "I like to think of artificial intelligence as the scientific apotheosis of a venerable cultural tradition." (<a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, p.&#160;34) "Artificial intelligence in one form or another is an idea that has pervaded Western intellectual history, a dream in urgent need of being realized." (<a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, p.&#160;xviii) "Our history is full of attempts—nutty, eerie, comical, earnest, legendary and real—to make artificial intelligences, to reproduce what is the essential us—bypassing the ordinary means. Back and forth between myth and reality, our imaginations supplying what our workshops couldn't, we have engaged for a long time in this odd form of self-reproduction." (<a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, p.&#160;3) She traces the desire back to its <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic" class="mw-redirect">Hellenistic</a> roots and calls it the urge to "forge the Gods." (<a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp.&#160;340–400)</li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b> The optimism referred to includes the predictions of early AI researchers (see <a href="/wiki/History_of_AI#The_optimism" title="History of AI" class="mw-redirect">optimism in the history of AI</a>) as well as the ideas of modern <a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">transhumanists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil" title="Ray Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a>.</li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b> The "setbacks" referred to include the <a href="/wiki/AI_winter#Machine_translation_and_the_ALPAC_report_of_1966" title="AI winter">ALPAC report</a> of 1966, the abandonment of <a href="/wiki/Perceptrons" title="Perceptrons" class="mw-redirect">perceptrons</a> in 1970, <a href="/wiki/AI_winter#The_Lighthill_report" title="AI winter">the Lighthill Report</a> of 1973 and the <a href="/wiki/AI_winter#The_collapse_of_the_Lisp_machine_market_in_1987" title="AI winter">collapse of the lisp machine market</a> in 1987.</li>
<li id="cite_note-AI_widely_used-8">^ <a href="#cite_ref-AI_widely_used_8-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AI_widely_used_8-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> AI applications widely used behind the scenes:
<ul>

</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Fragmentation_of_AI-9"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Fragmentation_of_AI_9-0">^</a></b> Pamela <a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck (2004</a>, pp.&#160;424) writes of "the rough shattering of AI in subfields—vision, natural language, decision theory, genetic algorithms, robotics ... and these with own sub-subfield—that would hardly have anything to say to each other."</li>
<li id="cite_note-Problems_of_AI-10">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Problems_of_AI_10-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Problems_of_AI_10-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> This list of intelligent traits is based on the topics covered by the major AI textbooks, including:

<li id="cite_note-General_intelligence-11">^ <a href="#cite_ref-General_intelligence_11-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-General_intelligence_11-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> General intelligence (<a href="/wiki/Strong_AI" title="Strong AI">strong AI</a>) is discussed in popular introductions to AI:

<li id="cite_note-Cult_images_as_artificial_intelligence-13"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Cult_images_as_artificial_intelligence_13-0">^</a></b> <a href="/wiki/Cult_image" title="Cult image">Cult images</a> as artificial intelligence:
<ul>

<li><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck (2004</a>, pp.&#160;6–9)</li>
</ul>
These were the first machines to be believed to have true intelligence and consciousness. <a href="/wiki/Hermes_Trismegistus" title="Hermes Trismegistus">Hermes Trismegistus</a> expressed the common belief that with these statues, craftsman had reproduced "the true nature of the gods", their <i>sensus</i> and <i>spiritus</i>. McCorduck makes the connection between sacred automatons and <a href="/wiki/613_Commandments" title="613 Commandments" class="mw-redirect">Mosaic law</a> (developed around the same time), which expressly forbids the worship of robots (<a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp.&#160;6–9)</li>

<li id="cite_note-AI.27s_immediate_precursors-19">^ <a href="#cite_ref-AI.27s_immediate_precursors_19-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AI.27s_immediate_precursors_19-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> AI's immediate precursors:
<ul>
<li><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp.&#160;51–107</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFCrevier1993">Crevier 1993</a>, pp.&#160;27–32</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, pp.&#160;15, 940</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFMoravec1988">Moravec 1988</a>, p.&#160;3</li>
</ul>
See also <a href="/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#Cybernetics_and_early_neural_networks" title="History of artificial intelligence">Cybernetics and early neural networks</a> (in <a href="/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence" title="History of artificial intelligence">History of artificial intelligence</a>). Among the researchers who laid the foundations of AI were <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Von_Neumann" title="John Von Neumann" class="mw-redirect">John Von Neumann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Wiener" title="Norbert Wiener">Norbert Wiener</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claude_Shannon" title="Claude Shannon">Claude Shannon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warren_McCullough" title="Warren McCullough" class="mw-redirect">Warren McCullough</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Pitts" title="Walter Pitts">Walter Pitts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Donald_Hebb" title="Donald Hebb" class="mw-redirect">Donald Hebb</a>.</li>
<li id="cite_note-Dartmouth_conference-20"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Dartmouth_conference_20-0">^</a></b> <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_conference" title="Dartmouth conference" class="mw-redirect">Dartmouth conference</a>:
<ul>
<li><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp.&#160;111–136</li>

</ul>

<li id="cite_note-Psychological_evidence_of_sub-symbolic_reasoning-41"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Psychological_evidence_of_sub-symbolic_reasoning_41-0">^</a></b> Psychological evidence of sub-symbolic reasoning:
<ul>
<li><a href="#CITEREFWasonShapiro1966">Wason &amp; Shapiro (1966)</a> showed that people do poorly on completely abstract problems, but if the problem is restated to allow the use of intuitive <a href="/wiki/Social_intelligence" title="Social intelligence">social intelligence</a>, performance dramatically improves. (See <a href="/wiki/Wason_selection_task" title="Wason selection task">Wason selection task</a>)</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFKahnemanSlovicTversky1982">Kahneman, Slovic &amp; Tversky (1982)</a> have shown that people are terrible at elementary problems that involve uncertain reasoning. (See <a href="/wiki/List_of_cognitive_biases" title="List of cognitive biases">list of cognitive biases</a> for several examples).</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFLakoffN.C3.BA.C3.B1ez2000">Lakoff &amp; Núñez (2000)</a> have controversially argued that even our skills at mathematics depend on knowledge and skills that come from "the body", i.e. sensorimotor and perceptual skills. (See <a href="/wiki/Where_Mathematics_Comes_From" title="Where Mathematics Comes From">Where Mathematics Comes From</a>)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Knowledge_representation-42"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Knowledge_representation_42-0">^</a></b> <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_representation" title="Knowledge representation" class="mw-redirect">Knowledge representation</a>:

<li id="cite_note-Qualification_problem-49"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Qualification_problem_49-0">^</a></b> <a href="/wiki/Qualification_problem" title="Qualification problem">Qualification problem</a>:
<ul>
<li><a href="#CITEREFMcCarthyHayes1969">McCarthy &amp; Hayes 1969</a></li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears from February 2011">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
</ul>
While McCarthy was primarily concerned with issues in the logical representation of actions, <a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a> apply the term to the more general issue of default reasoning in the vast network of assumptions underlying all our commonsense knowledge.</li>
<li id="cite_note-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic-50">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic_50-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Default_reasoning_and_non-monotonic_logic_50-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> Default reasoning and <a href="/wiki/Default_logic" title="Default logic">default logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-monotonic_logic" title="Non-monotonic logic">non-monotonic logics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circumscription_(logic)" title="Circumscription (logic)">circumscription</a>, <a href="/wiki/Closed_world_assumption" title="Closed world assumption">closed world assumption</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abductive_reasoning" title="Abductive reasoning">abduction</a> (Poole <i>et al.</i> places abduction under "default reasoning". Luger <i>et al.</i> places this under "uncertain reasoning"):
<ul>
<li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, pp.&#160;354–360,</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFPooleMackworthGoebel1998">Poole, Mackworth &amp; Goebel 1998</a>, pp.&#160;248–256, 323–335,</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFLugerStubblefield2004">Luger &amp; Stubblefield 2004</a>, pp.&#160;335–363,</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFNilsson1998">Nilsson 1998</a>, ~18.3.3</li>
</ul>

<li id="cite_note-Artificial_consciousness-75"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Artificial_consciousness_75-0">^</a></b> <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Edelman" title="Gerald Edelman">Gerald Edelman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Igor_Aleksander" title="Igor Aleksander">Igor Aleksander</a> and others have both argued that <a href="/wiki/Artificial_consciousness" title="Artificial consciousness">artificial consciousness</a> is required for strong AI. (<a href="#CITEREFAleksander1995">Aleksander 1995</a>; <a href="#CITEREFEdelman2007">Edelman 2007</a>)</li>
<li id="cite_note-Brain_simulation-76">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Brain_simulation_76-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brain_simulation_76-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="/wiki/Artificial_brain" title="Artificial brain">Artificial brain</a> arguments: AI requires a simulation of the operation of the human brain
<ul>
<li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, p.&#160;957</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFCrevier1993">Crevier 1993</a>, pp.&#160;271 and 279</li>
</ul>
A few of the people who make some form of the argument:
<ul>
<li><a href="#CITEREFMoravec1988">Moravec 1988</a></li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFKurzweil2005">Kurzweil 2005</a>, p.&#160;262</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFHawkinsBlakeslee2005">Hawkins &amp; Blakeslee 2005</a></li>
</ul>
The most extreme form of this argument (the brain replacement scenario) was put forward by <a href="/wiki/Clark_Glymour" title="Clark Glymour">Clark Glymour</a> in the mid-70s and was touched on by <a href="/wiki/Zenon_Pylyshyn" title="Zenon Pylyshyn">Zenon Pylyshyn</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Searle" title="John Searle">John Searle</a> in 1980.</li>
<li id="cite_note-AI_complete-77"><b><a href="#cite_ref-AI_complete_77-0">^</a></b> <a href="/wiki/AI_complete" title="AI complete" class="mw-redirect">AI complete</a>: <a href="#CITEREFShapiro1992">Shapiro 1992</a>, p.&#160;9</li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b> <a href="/wiki/Nils_Nilsson_(researcher)" title="Nils Nilsson (researcher)">Nils Nilsson</a> writes: "Simply put, there is wide disagreement in the field about what AI is all about" (<a href="#CITEREFNilsson1983">Nilsson 1983</a>, p.&#160;10).</li>
<li id="cite_note-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general-79">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general_79-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Biological_intelligence_vs._intelligence_in_general_79-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> Biological intelligence vs. intelligence in general:
<ul>
<li><a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig 2003</a>, pp.&#160;2–3, who make the analogy with <a href="/wiki/Aeronautical_engineering" title="Aeronautical engineering" class="mw-redirect">aeronautical engineering</a>.</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFMcCorduck2004">McCorduck 2004</a>, pp.&#160;100–101, who writes that there are "two major branches of artificial intelligence: one aimed at producing intelligent behavior regardless of how it was accomplioshed, and the other aimed at modeling intelligent processes found in nature, particularly human ones."</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFKolata1982">Kolata 1982</a>, a paper in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i>, which describes <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)">McCathy's</a> indifference to biological models. Kolata quotes McCarthy as writing: "This is AI, so we don't care if it's psychologically real"<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://books.google.com/books?id=PEkqAAAAMAAJ&amp;q=%22we+don't+care+if+it's+psychologically+real%22&amp;dq=%22we+don't+care+if+it's+psychologically+real%22&amp;output=html&amp;pgis=1">[1]</a>. McCarthy recently reiterated his position at the <a href="/wiki/AI@50" title="AI@50">AI@50</a> conference where he said "Artificial intelligence is not, by definition, simulation of human intelligence" (<a href="#CITEREFMaker2006">Maker 2006</a>).</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Neats_vs._scruffies-80">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Neats_vs._scruffies_80-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Neats_vs._scruffies_80-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="/wiki/Neats_vs._scruffies" title="Neats vs. scruffies">Neats vs. scruffies</a>:

<li><a href="#CITEREFNilsson1983">Nilsson 1983</a>, pp.&#160;10–11</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic-81">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic_81-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Symbolic_vs._sub-symbolic_81-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> Symbolic vs. sub-symbolic AI:

<li id="cite_note-Searle.27s_strong_AI-155"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Searle.27s_strong_AI_155-0">^</a></b> This version is from <a href="#CITEREFSearle1999">Searle (1999)</a>, and is also quoted in <a href="#CITEREFDennett1991">Dennett 1991</a>, p.&#160;435. Searle's original formulation was "The appropriately programmed computer really is a mind, in the sense that computers given the right programs can be literally said to understand and have other cognitive states." (<a href="#CITEREFSearle1980">Searle 1980</a>, p.&#160;1). Strong AI is defined similarly by <a href="#CITEREFRussellNorvig2003">Russell &amp; Norvig (2003</a>, p.&#160;947): "The assertion that machines could possibly act intelligently (or, perhaps better, act as if they were intelligent) is called the 'weak AI' hypothesis by philosophers, and the assertion that machines that do so are actually thinking (as opposed to simulating thinking) is called the 'strong AI' hypothesis."</li>
<li id="cite_note-Chinese_room-156"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Chinese_room_156-0">^</a></b> Searle's <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Room" title="Chinese Room" class="mw-redirect">Chinese Room</a> argument:

</ul>

</ul>

<li id="cite_note-Robot_rights-157"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Robot_rights_157-0">^</a></b> <a href="/wiki/Robot_rights" title="Robot rights" class="mw-redirect">Robot rights</a>:

<ul>
<li>TechCast Article Series, John Sagi, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.techcast.org/Upload/PDFs/634146249446122137_Consciousness-Sagifinalversion.pdf">Framing Consciousness</a></li>
